				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div role="note" class="hatnote">This article is about chemical elements. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_(disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" title="Heavy metal (disambiguation)">Heavy metal (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:261px;max-width:261px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Osmium_crystals.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A silvery thumbnail-size chunk of osmium with a highly irregular crystalline surface." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Osmium_crystals.jpg/259px-Osmium_crystals.jpg" width="259" height="166" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Osmium_crystals.jpg/389px-Osmium_crystals.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Osmium_crystals.jpg/518px-Osmium_crystals.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4824" data-file-height="3099" /></a></div>

<div style="clear:left"></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">
<center>Crystals of <a href="/wiki/Osmium" title="Osmium">osmium</a>, a heavy metal<br />
nearly twice as dense as <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></center>

<p><b>Heavy metals</b> are generally defined as <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metals</a> with relatively high <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">densities</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atomic_weight" class="mw-redirect" title="Atomic weight">atomic weights</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic numbers</a>. The criteria used, and whether <a href="/wiki/Metalloid" title="Metalloid">metalloids</a> are included, vary depending on the author and context. In <a href="/wiki/Metallurgy" title="Metallurgy">metallurgy</a>, for example, a heavy metal may be defined on the basis of density, whereas in physics the distinguishing criterion might be atomic number, while a chemist would likely be more concerned with <a href="/wiki/Chemical_property" title="Chemical property">chemical behaviour</a>. More specific definitions have been published, but none of these have been widely accepted. The definitions surveyed in this article encompass up to 96 out of the 118 <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical elements</a>; only <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bismuth" title="Bismuth">bismuth</a> meet all of them. Despite this lack of agreement, the term (plural or singular) is widely used in science. A density of more than 5&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup> is sometimes quoted as a commonly used criterion and is used in the body of this article.</p>
<p>The earliest known metals—common metals such as <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a>, and precious metals such as <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a>—are heavy metals. From 1809 onwards, <a href="/wiki/Light_metal" title="Light metal">light metals</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">titanium</a>, were discovered, as well as less well-known heavy metals including <a href="/wiki/Gallium" title="Gallium">gallium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thallium" title="Thallium">thallium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hafnium" title="Hafnium">hafnium</a>.</p>
<p>Some heavy metals are either essential nutrients (typically iron, <a href="/wiki/Cobalt" title="Cobalt">cobalt</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a>), or relatively harmless (such as <a href="/wiki/Ruthenium" title="Ruthenium">ruthenium</a>, silver, and <a href="/wiki/Indium" title="Indium">indium</a>), but can be toxic in larger amounts or certain forms. Other heavy metals, such as <a href="/wiki/Cadmium" title="Cadmium">cadmium</a>, mercury, and lead, are highly poisonous. Potential sources of heavy metal poisoning include <a href="/wiki/Tailings" title="Tailings">mining</a> and <a href="/wiki/Industrial_waste" title="Industrial waste">industrial wastes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_runoff" class="mw-redirect" title="Agricultural runoff">agricultural runoff</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chemical_hazard" title="Chemical hazard">occupational exposure</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_paint" title="Environmental impact of paint">paints</a> and <a href="/wiki/Treated_wood" class="mw-redirect" title="Treated wood">treated timber</a>.</p>
<p>Physical and chemical characterisations of heavy metals need to be treated with caution, as the metals involved are not always consistently defined. As well as being relatively dense, heavy metals tend to be less <a href="/wiki/Chemical_reaction" title="Chemical reaction">reactive</a> than lighter metals and have much less <a href="/wiki/Solubility" title="Solubility">soluble</a> <a href="/wiki/Sulfide" title="Sulfide">sulfides</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydroxide" title="Hydroxide">hydroxides</a>. While it is relatively easy to distinguish a heavy metal such as tungsten from a lighter metal such as <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a>, a few heavy metals such as zinc, mercury, and lead have some of the characteristics of lighter metals, and lighter metals such as <a href="/wiki/Beryllium" title="Beryllium">beryllium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scandium" title="Scandium">scandium</a>, and titanium have some of the characteristics of heavier metals.</p>
<p>Heavy metals are relatively scarce in the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Structure_of_the_Earth#Crust" title="Structure of the Earth">crust</a> but are present in many aspects of modern life. They are used in, for example, <a href="/wiki/Golf_club" title="Golf club">golf clubs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cars" class="mw-redirect" title="Cars">cars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antiseptic" title="Antiseptic">antiseptics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Self-cleaning_oven" title="Self-cleaning oven">self-cleaning ovens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plastic" title="Plastic">plastics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_panel" title="Solar panel">solar panels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mobile_phone" title="Mobile phone">mobile phones</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Particle_accelerator" title="Particle accelerator">particle accelerators</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<tr>
<td colspan="20" style="font-size:90%; padding-right:10px; padding-left:10px; text-align:left">This table shows the number of heavy metal criteria met by each metal, out of the ten criteria listed in this section i.e. two based on <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a>, three on <a href="/wiki/Atomic_weight" class="mw-redirect" title="Atomic weight">atomic weight</a>, two on <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a>, and three on chemical behaviour.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[n 1]</a></sup> It illustrates the lack of agreement surrounding the concept, with the possible exception of <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bismuth" title="Bismuth">bismuth</a>.<br />
<p>Six elements near the end of <a href="/wiki/Period_(periodic_table)" title="Period (periodic table)">periods</a> (rows) 4 to 7 sometimes considered metalloids are treated here as metals, including <a href="/wiki/Germanium" title="Germanium">germanium</a> (Ge), <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a> (As), <a href="/wiki/Selenium" title="Selenium">selenium</a> (Se), <a href="/wiki/Antimony" title="Antimony">antimony</a> (Sb), <a href="/wiki/Tellurium" title="Tellurium">tellurium</a> (Te), and <a href="/wiki/Astatine" title="Astatine">astatine</a> (At).<sup id="cite_ref-Vernon_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vernon-16">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[n 2]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Oganesson" title="Oganesson">Oganesson</a> (Og) is treated as a nonmetal.</p>
<p>Metals enclosed by a dashed line have (or, for At and elements 100–117, are predicted to have) densities of more than 5&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup>.</p>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>There is no widely agreed criterion-based definition of a heavy metal. Different meanings may be attached to the term, depending on the context. In <a href="/wiki/Metallurgy" title="Metallurgy">metallurgy</a>, for example, a heavy metal may be defined on the basis of <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[16]</a></sup> whereas in physics the distinguishing criterion might be <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[17]</a></sup> and a chemist would likely be more concerned with chemical behaviour.<sup id="cite_ref-Hawkes_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hawkes-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>Density criteria range from above 3.5&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup> to above 7&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-Duffus798_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Duffus798-2">[2]</a></sup> Atomic weight definitions can range from greater than <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a> (atomic weight 22.98);<sup id="cite_ref-Duffus798_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Duffus798-2">[2]</a></sup> greater than 40 (excluding <a href="/wiki/S-block" class="mw-redirect" title="S-block">s-</a> and <a href="/wiki/F-block" class="mw-redirect" title="F-block">f-block</a> metals, hence starting with <a href="/wiki/Scandium" title="Scandium">scandium</a>);<sup id="cite_ref-Rand_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rand-3">[3]</a></sup> or more than 200, i.e. from <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a> onwards.<sup id="cite_ref-Baldwin_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baldwin-4">[4]</a></sup> Atomic numbers of heavy metals are generally given as greater than 20 (<a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a>);<sup id="cite_ref-Duffus798_2-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Duffus798-2">[2]</a></sup> sometimes this is capped at 92 (<a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Lyman_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lyman-5">[5]</a></sup> Definitions based on atomic number have been criticised for including metals with low densities. For example, <a href="/wiki/Rubidium" title="Rubidium">rubidium</a> in <a href="/wiki/Alkali_metals" class="mw-redirect" title="Alkali metals">group (column) 1</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a> has an atomic number of 37 but a density of only 1.532&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup>, which is below the threshold figure used by other authors.<sup id="cite_ref-Duffus797_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Duffus797-20">[18]</a></sup> The same problem may occur with atomic weight based definitions.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>Criteria based on chemical behaviour or periodic table position have been used or suggested. The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Pharmacopeia" title="United States Pharmacopeia">United States Pharmacopeia</a> includes a test for heavy metals that involves precipitating metallic impurities as their coloured <a href="/wiki/Sulfide" title="Sulfide">sulfides</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-USP_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USP-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[n 3]</a></sup> In 1997, Stephen Hawkes, a chemistry professor writing in the context of fifty years' experience with the term, said it applied to "metals with insoluble sulfides and <a href="/wiki/Hydroxide" title="Hydroxide">hydroxides</a>, whose <a href="/wiki/Salt_(chemistry)" title="Salt (chemistry)">salts</a> produce colored solutions in water and whose <a href="/wiki/Coordination_complex" title="Coordination complex">complexes</a> are usually colored". On the basis of the metals he had seen referred to as heavy metals, he suggested it would useful to define them as (in general) all the metals in periodic table columns <a href="/wiki/Group_3_element" title="Group 3 element">3</a> to <a href="/wiki/Chalcogen" title="Chalcogen">16</a> that are in <a href="/wiki/Period_4" class="mw-redirect" title="Period 4">row 4</a> or greater, in other words, the <a href="/wiki/Transition_metal" title="Transition metal">transition metals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Post-transition_metal" title="Post-transition metal">post-transition metals</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hawkes_9-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hawkes-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[n 4]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Lanthanide" title="Lanthanide">lanthanides</a> satisfy Hawkes' three-part description; the status of the <a href="/wiki/Actinide" title="Actinide">actinides</a> is not completely settled.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[n 5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[n 6]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">biochemistry</a>, heavy metals are sometimes defined—on the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Lewis_acid" class="mw-redirect" title="Lewis acid">Lewis acid</a> (electronic pair acceptor) behaviour of their ions in aqueous solution—as class B and borderline metals.<sup id="cite_ref-Rain_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rain-46">[40]</a></sup> In this scheme, class A metal ions prefer <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> donors; class B ions prefer <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> donors; and borderline or ambivalent ions show either class A or B characteristics, depending on the circumstances.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[n 7]</a></sup> Class A metals, which tend to have low <a href="/wiki/Electronegativity" title="Electronegativity">electronegativity</a> and form bonds with large <a href="/wiki/Ionic_bond" class="mw-redirect" title="Ionic bond">ionic character</a>, are the <a href="/wiki/Alkali_metal" title="Alkali metal">alkali</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alkaline_earth" class="mw-redirect" title="Alkaline earth">alkaline earths</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Group_3_element" title="Group 3 element">group 3 metals</a>, and the lanthanides and actinides.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[n 8]</a></sup> Class B metals, which tend to have higher electronegativity and form bonds with considerable <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalent</a> character, are mainly the heavier transition and post-transition metals. Borderline metals largely comprise the lighter transition and post-transition metals (plus <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antimony" title="Antimony">antimony</a>). The distinction between the class A metals and the other two categories is sharp.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[44]</a></sup> A frequently cited proposal<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[n 9]</a></sup> to use these classification categories instead of the more evocative<sup id="cite_ref-Nieboer_1980_4_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nieboer_1980_4-10">[10]</a></sup> name <i>heavy metal</i> has not been widely adopted.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[46]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="List_of_heavy_metals_based_on_density">List of heavy metals based on density</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metals&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: List of heavy metals based on density">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A density of more than 5&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup> is sometimes mentioned as a common heavy metal defining factor<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[47]</a></sup> and, in the absence of a unanimous definition, is used to populate this list and (unless otherwise stated) guide the remainder of the article. Metalloids meeting the applicable criteria–arsenic and antimony for example—are sometimes counted as heavy metals, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Environmental_chemistry" title="Environmental chemistry">environmental chemistry</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[48]</a></sup> as is the case here. <a href="/wiki/Selenium" title="Selenium">Selenium</a> (density 4.8&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup>)<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[49]</a></sup> is also included in the list. It falls marginally short of the density criterion and is less commonly recognised as a metalloid<sup id="cite_ref-Vernon_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vernon-16">[15]</a></sup> but has a waterborne chemistry similar in some respects to that of arsenic and antimony.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[50]</a></sup> Other metals sometimes classified or treated as "heavy" metals, such as <a href="/wiki/Beryllium" title="Beryllium">beryllium</a><sup id="cite_ref-Ikehata_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ikehata-60">[51]</a></sup> (density 1.8 gm/cm<sup>3</sup>),<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[52]</a></sup> aluminium<sup id="cite_ref-Ikehata_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ikehata-60">[51]</a></sup> (2.7 gm/cm<sup>3</sup>),<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[53]</a></sup> calcium<sup id="cite_ref-podsiki_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-podsiki-63">[54]</a></sup> (1.55 gm/cm<sup>3</sup>),<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[55]</a></sup> and barium<sup id="cite_ref-podsiki_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-podsiki-63">[54]</a></sup> (3.6 gm/cm<sup>3</sup>)<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[56]</a></sup> are here treated as <a href="/wiki/Light_metal" title="Light metal">light metals</a> and, in general, are not further considered.</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="max-width: 800px; border-collapse: collapse;" summary="This table lists all 84 heavy metals grouped informally into six categories: four categories for those mainly produced by commercial mining and two categories for those mainly produced by nuclear synthesis. Each of the six categories begins with a micro periodic table highlighting the elements in the category. The title of the periodic table names the category; its caption explains the category. After the periodic table, each category includes a list of elements. Some element names are followed by footnote markers which are explained at the end of the table.">

<td style="text-align:center; background:#d8d8d8; padding:2px 4px; font-size:90%; line-height:110%; border:none;"><b>Strategic (30)</b></td>

<tr>
<td style="margin:0; padding:0;">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins_and_use_of_the_term">Origins and use of the term</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metals&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Origins and use of the term">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The heaviness of <a href="/wiki/Native_metal" title="Native metal">naturally occurring metals</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Telluric_iron" title="Telluric iron">iron</a> may have been noticed in <a href="/wiki/Prehistory" title="Prehistory">prehistory</a> and, in light of their <a href="/wiki/Malleable" class="mw-redirect" title="Malleable">malleability</a>, led to the first attempts to craft metal ornaments, tools, and weapons.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[63]</a></sup> All metals discovered from then until 1809 had relatively high densities; their heaviness was regarded as a singularly distinguishing criterion.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>From 1809 onwards, light metals such as sodium, potassium, and <a href="/wiki/Strontium" title="Strontium">strontium</a> were isolated. Their low densities challenged conventional wisdom and it was proposed to refer to them as <i><a href="/wiki/Metalloid" title="Metalloid">metalloids</a></i> (meaning "resembling metals in form or appearance").<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[65]</a></sup> This suggestion was ignored; the new elements came to be recognised as metals, and the term metalloid was then used to refer to nonmetallic elements and, later, elements that were hard to describe as either metals or nonmetals.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>An early use of the term "heavy metal" dates from 1817, when the German chemist <a href="/wiki/Leopold_Gmelin" title="Leopold Gmelin">Leopold Gmelin</a> divided the elements into nonmetals, light metals, and heavy metals.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[67]</a></sup> Light metals had densities of 0.860–5.0&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup>; heavy metals 5.308–22.000.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[68]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[n 10]</a></sup> The term later became associated with elements of high atomic weight or high atomic number.<sup id="cite_ref-Duffus797_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Duffus797-20">[18]</a></sup> It is sometimes used interchangeably with the term <i>heavy element</i>. For example, in discussing the history of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_chemistry" title="Nuclear chemistry">nuclear chemistry</a>, Magee<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[69]</a></sup> notes that the actinides were once thought to represent a new heavy element transition group whereas <a href="/wiki/Glenn_T._Seaborg" title="Glenn T. Seaborg">Seaborg</a> and co-workers, "favoured&#160;... a heavy metal <a href="/wiki/Lanthanide" title="Lanthanide">rare-earth</a> like series&#160;...". In <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a>, however, a heavy element is any element heavier than <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[70]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metals&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Criticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2002, Scottish toxicologist <a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Henderson_Duffus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="John Henderson Duffus (page does not exist)">John Duffus</a> reviewed the definitions used over the previous 60 years and concluded they were so diverse as to effectively render the term meaningless.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[71]</a></sup> Along with this finding, the heavy metal status of some metals is occasionally challenged on the grounds that they are too light, or are involved in biological processes, or rarely constitute environmental hazards. Examples include scandium (too light);<sup id="cite_ref-Duffus797_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Duffus797-20">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[72]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vanadium" title="Vanadium">vanadium</a> to <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a> (biological processes);<sup id="cite_ref-Housecroft_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Housecroft-83">[73]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Rhodium" title="Rhodium">rhodium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indium" title="Indium">indium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Osmium" title="Osmium">osmium</a> (too rare).<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Popularity">Popularity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metals&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Popularity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Despite its questionable meaning, references to the term <i>heavy metal</i> appear regularly in scientific literature. A 2010 study found that it had been increasingly used and seemed to have become part of the language of science.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[75]</a></sup> It is said to be an acceptable term, given its convenience and familiarity, as long as it is accompanied by a strict definition.<sup id="cite_ref-Rain_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rain-46">[40]</a></sup> The counterparts to the heavy metals, the <i>light metals</i>, are alluded to by <a href="/wiki/The_Minerals,_Metals_%26_Materials_Society" class="mw-redirect" title="The Minerals, Metals &amp; Materials Society">The Minerals, Metals and Materials Society</a> as including "aluminium, <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beryllium" title="Beryllium">beryllium</a>, titanium, <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">lithium</a>, and other reactive metals."<sup id="cite_ref-TMS_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TMS-86">[76]</a></sup> The named metals have densities of 0.534 to 4.54&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_role">Biological role</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metals&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Biological role">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Essential_element" class="mw-redirect" title="Essential element">Essential element</a></div>
<p>Trace amounts of some heavy metals, mostly in period 4, are required for certain biological processes. These are iron and copper (<a href="/wiki/Blood#Oxygen_transport" title="Blood">oxygen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electron_transport_chain" title="Electron transport chain">electron transport</a>); <a href="/wiki/Cobalt" title="Cobalt">cobalt</a> (<a href="/wiki/Vitamin_B12" title="Vitamin B12">complex syntheses and cell metabolism</a>); zinc (<a href="/wiki/Hydroxylation" title="Hydroxylation">hydroxylation</a>);<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[82]</a></sup> vanadium and <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a> (<a href="/wiki/Cofactor_(biochemistry)" title="Cofactor (biochemistry)">enzyme regulation</a> or functioning); <a href="/wiki/Chromium" title="Chromium">chromium</a> (<a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> utilisation); nickel (<a href="/wiki/Cell_growth" title="Cell growth">cell growth</a>); arsenic (metabolic growth in some animals and possibly in humans) and selenium (<a href="/wiki/Antioxidant" title="Antioxidant">antioxidant</a> functioning and <a href="/wiki/Hormone" title="Hormone">hormone</a> production).<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[83]</a></sup> Periods 5 and 6 contain fewer essential heavy metals, consistent with the general pattern that heavier elements tend to be less abundant and that scarcer elements are less likely to be nutritionally essential.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[84]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Period_5" class="mw-redirect" title="Period 5">period 5</a>, <a href="/wiki/Molybdenum" title="Molybdenum">molybdenum</a> is required for the <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalysis</a> of <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">redox</a> reactions; <a href="/wiki/Cadmium" title="Cadmium">cadmium</a> is used by some marine <a href="/wiki/Diatom" title="Diatom">diatoms</a> for the same purpose; and <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a> may be required for growth in a few species.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[85]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Period_6" class="mw-redirect" title="Period 6">period 6</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a> is required by some bacteria for <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_process" class="mw-redirect" title="Metabolic process">metabolic processes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[86]</a></sup> An average 70&#160;kg <a href="/wiki/Composition_of_the_human_body" title="Composition of the human body">human body</a> is about 0.01% heavy metals (~7&#160;g, equivalent to the weight of two dried peas, with iron at 4&#160;g, zinc at 2.5&#160;g, and lead at 0.12&#160;g comprising the three main constituents), 2% light metals (~1.4&#160;kg, the weight of a bottle of wine) and nearly 98% nonmetals (mostly <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[87]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[n 15]</a></sup></p>
<p>A deficiency of any of these period 4–6 essential heavy metals may increase susceptibility to <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_poisoning" class="mw-redirect" title="Heavy metal poisoning">heavy metal poisoning</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[88]</a></sup> A few non-essential heavy metals have also been observed to have biological effects. <a href="/wiki/Gallium" title="Gallium">Gallium</a>, germanium (a metalloid), indium, and most lanthanides can stimulate metabolism, and titanium promotes growth in plants<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[89]</a></sup> (though it is not always considered a heavy metal).</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Toxicity">Toxicity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metals&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Toxicity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<dl>
<dd><i>The focus of this section is mainly on the more serious toxic effects of heavy metals, including cancer, brain damage or death, rather than the harm they may cause to one more of the skin, lungs, stomach, kidneys, liver, or heart. For more specific information see <a href="/wiki/Metal_toxicity" title="Metal toxicity">Metal toxicity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toxic_heavy_metal" title="Toxic heavy metal">Toxic heavy metal</a>, or the articles on individual elements or compounds.</i></dd>
</dl>
<p>Heavy metals are often assumed to be highly toxic or damaging to the environment.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[90]</a></sup> Some are, while certain others are toxic only if taken in excess or encountered in certain forms.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_heavy_metals">Environmental heavy metals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metals&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Environmental heavy metals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Chromium, arsenic, cadmium, mercury, and lead have the greatest potential to cause harm on account of their extensive use, the <a href="/wiki/Toxicity" title="Toxicity">toxicity</a> of some of their combined or elemental forms, and their widespread distribution in the environment.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[91]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hexavalent_chromium" title="Hexavalent chromium">Hexavalent chromium</a>, for example, is highly toxic as are mercury vapour and many mercury compounds.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[92]</a></sup> These five elements have a strong affinity for sulfur; in the human body they usually bind, via <a href="/wiki/Thiol" title="Thiol">thiol</a> groups (–SH), to <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> responsible for controlling the speed of metabolic reactions. The resulting sulfur-metal bonds inhibit the proper functioning of the enzymes involved; human health deteriorates, sometimes fatally.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[93]</a></sup> Chromium (in its hexavalent form) and arsenic are <a href="/wiki/Carcinogen" title="Carcinogen">carcinogens</a>; cadmium causes a <a href="/wiki/Itai-itai_disease" title="Itai-itai disease">degenerative bone disease</a>; and mercury and lead damage the <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a>.</p>

<p>Lead is the most prevalent heavy metal contaminant.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[94]</a></sup> Levels in the aquatic environments of industrialised societies have been estimated to be two to three times those of pre-industrial levels.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[95]</a></sup> As a component of <a href="/wiki/Tetraethyl_lead" class="mw-redirect" title="Tetraethyl lead">tetraethyl lead</a>, <span class="chemf nowrap">(CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
3</span>CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
4</span>Pb</span>, it was used extensively in <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a> during the 1930s–1970s.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[96]</a></sup> Although the use of leaded gasoline was largely phased out in North America by 1996, soils next to roads built before this time retain high lead concentrations.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[97]</a></sup> Later research demonstrated a statistically significant correlation between the usage rate of leaded gasoline and violent crime in the United States; taking into account a 22-year time lag (for the average age of violent criminals), the violent crime curve virtually tracked the lead exposure curve.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other heavy metals noted for their potentially hazardous nature, usually as toxic environmental pollutants, include manganese (central nervous system damage);<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[99]</a></sup> cobalt and <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a> (carcinogens);<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[100]</a></sup> copper,<sup id="cite_ref-Tokar_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tokar-116">[101]</a></sup> zinc,<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[102]</a></sup> selenium<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[103]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a><sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[104]</a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/Endocrine_system" title="Endocrine system">endocrine</a> disruption, <a href="/wiki/Congenital_disorder" title="Congenital disorder">congenital disorders</a>, or general toxic effects in fish, plants, birds, or other aquatic organisms); tin, as <a href="/wiki/Organotin" class="mw-redirect" title="Organotin">organotin</a> (central nervous system damage);<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[105]</a></sup> antimony (a suspected carcinogen);<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[106]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Thallium" title="Thallium">thallium</a> (central nervous system damage).<sup id="cite_ref-Tokar_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tokar-116">[101]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[n 16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[n 17]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nutritionally_essential_heavy_metals">Nutritionally essential heavy metals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metals&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Nutritionally essential heavy metals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Heavy metals essential for life can be toxic if taken in excess; some have notably toxic forms. <a href="/wiki/Vanadium_pentoxide" class="mw-redirect" title="Vanadium pentoxide">Vanadium pentoxide</a> (V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>) is carcinogenic in animals and, when inhaled, causes <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> damage.<sup id="cite_ref-Tokar_116-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tokar-116">[101]</a></sup> The purple <a href="/wiki/Permanganate" title="Permanganate">permanganate</a> ion MnO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">–<br />
4</span> is a <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kidney" title="Kidney">kidney</a> poison.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[110]</a></sup> Ingesting more than 0.5 grams of iron can induce cardiac collapse; such overdoses most commonly occur in children and may result in death within 24 hours.<sup id="cite_ref-Tokar_116-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tokar-116">[101]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nickel_carbonyl" class="mw-redirect" title="Nickel carbonyl">Nickel carbonyl</a> (Ni<sub>2</sub>(CO)<sub>4</sub>), at 30 parts per million, can cause respiratory failure, brain damage and death.<sup id="cite_ref-Tokar_116-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tokar-116">[101]</a></sup> Imbibing a gram or more of <a href="/wiki/Copper_sulfate" title="Copper sulfate">copper sulfate</a> (Cu(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>) can be fatal; survivors may be left with major organ damage.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[111]</a></sup> More than five milligrams of selenium is highly toxic; this is roughly ten times the 0.45 milligram recommended maximum daily intake;<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[112]</a></sup> long-term poisoning can have paralytic effects.<sup id="cite_ref-Tokar_116-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tokar-116">[101]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[n 18]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_heavy_metals">Other heavy metals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metals&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Other heavy metals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A few other non-essential heavy metals have one or more toxic forms. Kidney failure and fatalities have been recorded arising from the ingestion of germanium dietary supplements (~15 to 300&#160;g in total consumed over a period of two months to three years).<sup id="cite_ref-Tokar_116-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tokar-116">[101]</a></sup> Exposure to <a href="/wiki/Osmium_tetroxide" title="Osmium tetroxide">osmium tetroxide</a> (OsO<sub>4</sub>) may cause permanent eye damage and can lead to respiratory failure<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[114]</a></sup> and death.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[115]</a></sup> Indium salts are toxic if more than few milligrams are ingested and will affect the kidneys, liver, and heart.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[116]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cisplatin" title="Cisplatin">Cisplatin</a> (PtCl<sub>2</sub>(NH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>), which is an important drug used to <a href="/wiki/Chemotherapy" title="Chemotherapy">kill cancer cells</a>, is also a kidney and nerve poison.<sup id="cite_ref-Tokar_116-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tokar-116">[101]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bismuth" title="Bismuth">Bismuth</a> compounds can cause liver damage if taken in excess; insoluble uranium compounds, as well as the dangerous <a href="/wiki/Radiation_damage" title="Radiation damage">radiation</a> they emit, can cause permanent kidney damage.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[117]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Exposure_sources">Exposure sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metals&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Exposure sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Heavy metals can degrade air, water, and soil quality, and subsequently cause health issues in plants, animals, and people, when they become concentrated as a result of industrial activities.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[118]</a></sup> Common sources of heavy metals in this context include mining and industrial wastes; vehicle emissions; <a href="/wiki/Lead%E2%80%93acid_battery" title="Lead–acid battery">lead-acid batteries</a>; fertilisers; <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_paint" title="Environmental impact of paint">paints</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Treated_wood" class="mw-redirect" title="Treated wood">treated timber</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[119]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pipe_(fluid_conveyance)#Materials" title="Pipe (fluid conveyance)">aging water supply infrastructure</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[120]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Microplastics" title="Microplastics">microplastics</a> floating in the world's oceans.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[121]</a></sup> Recent examples of heavy metal contamination and health risks include the occurrence of <a href="/wiki/Minamata_disease" title="Minamata disease">Minamata disease</a>, in Japan (1932–1968; lawsuits ongoing as of 2016);<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[122]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Bento_Rodrigues_dam_disaster" title="Bento Rodrigues dam disaster">Bento Rodrigues dam disaster</a> in Brazil,<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[123]</a></sup> and high levels of lead in drinking water supplied to the residents of <a href="/wiki/Flint_water_crisis" title="Flint water crisis">Flint</a>, Michigan, in the north-east of the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[124]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation.2C_abundance.2C_occurrence.2C_and_extraction">Formation, abundance, occurrence, and extraction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metals&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Formation, abundance, occurrence, and extraction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<td colspan="20" style="text-align:left; padding-left: 10px;">Heavy metals left of the dividing line occur (or are sourced) mainly as <a href="/wiki/Goldschmidt_classification#Lithophile_elements" title="Goldschmidt classification">lithophiles</a>; those to the right, as <a href="/wiki/Goldschmidt_classification#Chalcophile_elements" title="Goldschmidt classification">chalcophiles</a> except gold (a <a href="/wiki/Goldschmidt_classification#Siderophile_elements" title="Goldschmidt classification">siderophile</a>) and tin (a lithophile).</td>

<p>Heavy metals up to the <a href="/wiki/Iron_peak" title="Iron peak">vicinity of iron</a> (in the periodic table) are largely made via <a href="/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis" title="Stellar nucleosynthesis">stellar nucleosynthesis</a>. In this process, lighter elements from hydrogen to <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a> undergo successive <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">fusion</a> reactions inside stars, releasing light and heat and forming heavier elements with higher atomic numbers.<sup id="cite_ref-Cox_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cox-147">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p>Heavier heavy metals are not usually formed this way since fusion reactions involving such nuclei would consume rather than release energy.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[129]</a></sup> Rather, they are largely synthesised (from elements with a lower atomic number) by <a href="/wiki/Neutron_capture" title="Neutron capture">neutron capture</a>, with the two main modes of this repetitive capture being the <a href="/wiki/S-process" title="S-process">s-process</a> and the <a href="/wiki/R-process" title="R-process">r-process</a>. In the s-process ("s" stands for "slow"), singular captures are separated by years or decades, allowing the less stable nuclei to <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[130]</a></sup> while in the r-process ("rapid"), captures happen faster than nuclei can decay. Therefore, the s-process takes a more or less clear path: for example, stable cadmium-110 nuclei are successively bombarded by free neutrons inside a star until they form cadmium-115 nuclei which are unstable and decay to form indium-115 (which is nearly stable, with a half-life <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7004300000000000000♠</span>30<span style="margin-left:.25em;">000</span></span> times the age of the universe). These nuclei capture neutrons and form indium-116, which is unstable, and decays to form tin-116, and so on.<sup id="cite_ref-Cox_147-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cox-147">[128]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[131]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[n 20]</a></sup> In contrast, there is no such path in the r-process. The s-process stops at bismuth due to the short half-lives of the next two elements, polonium, and astatine, which decay to bismuth or lead. The r-process is so fast it can skip this zone of instability and go on to create heavier elements such as <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a> and uranium.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[133]</a></sup></p>
<p>Heavy metals condense in planets as a result of stellar evolution and destruction processes. Stars lose much of their mass when it is <a href="/wiki/Stellar_mass_loss" title="Stellar mass loss">ejected</a> late in their lifetimes, and sometimes thereafter as a result of a <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">neutron star</a> merger,<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[134]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[n 21]</a></sup> thereby increasing the abundance of elements heavier than helium in the <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a>. When gravitational attraction causes this matter to coalesce and collapse <a href="/wiki/Nebular_hypothesis" title="Nebular hypothesis">new stars and planets are formed</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[136]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Earth's crust is made of approximately 5% of heavy metals by weight, with iron comprising 95% of this quantity. Light metals (~20%) and nonmetals (~75%) make up the other 95% of the crust.<sup id="cite_ref-Lide_143-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lide-143">[125]</a></sup> Despite their overall scarcity, heavy metals can become concentrated in economically extractable quantities as a result of <a href="/wiki/Mountain_formation" title="Mountain formation">mountain building</a>, <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">erosion</a>, or other <a href="/wiki/Geomorphology" title="Geomorphology">geological processes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[137]</a></sup></p>
<p>Heavy metals are primarily found as <a href="/wiki/Goldschmidt_classification#Lithophile_elements" title="Goldschmidt classification">lithophiles</a> (rock-loving) or <a href="/wiki/Goldschmidt_classification#Chalcophile_elements" title="Goldschmidt classification">chalcophiles</a> (ore-loving). Lithophile heavy metals are mainly f-block elements and the more reactive of the <a href="/wiki/D-block" class="mw-redirect" title="D-block">d-block</a> elements. They have a strong affinity for oxygen and mostly exist as relatively low density <a href="/wiki/Silicate_minerals" title="Silicate minerals">silicate minerals</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[138]</a></sup> Chalcophile heavy metals are mainly the less reactive d-block elements, and period 4–6 <a href="/wiki/P-block" class="mw-redirect" title="P-block">p-block</a> metals and metalloids. They are usually found in (insoluble) <a href="/wiki/Sulfide_minerals" title="Sulfide minerals">sulfide minerals</a>. Being denser than the lithophiles, hence sinking lower into the crust at the time of its solidification, the chalcophiles tend to be less abundant than the lithophiles.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[139]</a></sup></p>
<p>On the other hand, gold is a <a href="/wiki/Goldschmidt_classification#Siderophile_elements" title="Goldschmidt classification">siderophile</a>, or iron-loving element. It does not readily form compounds with either oxygen or sulfur.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[140]</a></sup> At the time of the <a href="/wiki/Formation_of_the_Earth" class="mw-redirect" title="Formation of the Earth">Earth's formation</a>, and as the most <a href="/wiki/Noble_metal" title="Noble metal">noble</a> (inert) of metals, gold sank into the <a href="/wiki/Structure_of_the_Earth#Core" title="Structure of the Earth">core</a> due to its tendency to form high-density metallic alloys. Consequently, it is a relatively rare metal.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[141]</a></sup> Some other (less) noble heavy metals—molybdenum, <a href="/wiki/Rhenium" title="Rhenium">rhenium</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Platinum_group_metal" class="mw-redirect" title="Platinum group metal">platinum group metals</a> (<a href="/wiki/Ruthenium" title="Ruthenium">ruthenium</a>, rhodium, <a href="/wiki/Palladium" title="Palladium">palladium</a>, osmium, <a href="/wiki/Iridium" title="Iridium">iridium</a>, and platinum), germanium, and tin—can be counted as siderophiles but only in terms of their primary occurrence in the Earth (core, <a href="/wiki/Structure_of_the_Earth#Mantle" title="Structure of the Earth">mantle</a> and crust), rather the crust. These metals otherwise occur in the crust, in small quantities, chiefly as chalcophiles (less so in their <a href="/wiki/Native_metal" title="Native metal">native form</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[142]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[n 22]</a></sup></p>
<p>Concentrations of heavy metals below the crust are generally higher, with most being found in the largely iron-silicon-nickel core. <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">Platinum</a>, for example, comprises approximately 1 part per billion of the crust whereas its concentration in the core is thought to be nearly 6,000 times higher.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[143]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[144]</a></sup> Recent speculation suggests that uranium (and thorium) in the core may generate a substantial amount of the heat that drives <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">plate tectonics</a> and (ultimately) sustains the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Earth's magnetic field">Earth's magnetic field</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[145]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[n 23]</a></sup></p>
<p>The winning of heavy metals from their ores is a complex function of ore type, the chemical properties of the metals involved, and the economics of various extraction methods. Different countries and refineries may use different processes, including those that differ from the brief outlines listed here.</p>
<p>Broadly speaking, and with some exceptions, lithophile heavy metals can be extracted from their ores by <a href="/wiki/Electrowinning" title="Electrowinning">electrical</a> or <a href="/wiki/Chemical_reduction" class="mw-redirect" title="Chemical reduction">chemical treatments</a>, while chalcophile heavy metals are obtained by <a href="/wiki/Roasting_(metallurgy)" title="Roasting (metallurgy)">roasting</a> their sulphide ores to yield the corresponding oxides, and then heating these to obtain the raw metals.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[147]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[n 24]</a></sup> Radium occurs in quantities too small to be economically mined and is instead obtained from spent <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel" title="Nuclear fuel">nuclear fuels</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[150]</a></sup> The chalcophile platinum group metals (PGM) mainly occur in small (mixed) quantities with other chalcophile ores. The ores involved need to be <a href="/wiki/Smelting" title="Smelting">smelted</a>, roasted, and then <a href="/wiki/Leaching_(metallurgy)" title="Leaching (metallurgy)">leached</a> with <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a> to produce a residue of PGM. This is chemically refined to obtain the individual metals in their pure forms.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[151]</a></sup> Compared to other metals, PGM are expensive due to their scarcity<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[152]</a></sup> and high production costs.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[153]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gold, a siderophile, is most commonly recovered by dissolving the ores in which it is found in a <a href="/wiki/Gold_cyanidation" title="Gold cyanidation">cyanide solution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[154]</a></sup> The gold forms a dicyanoaurate(I), for example: 2 Au + <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">H<sub>2</sub>O</a> +½ O<sub>2</sub> + <a href="/wiki/Potassium_cyanide" title="Potassium cyanide">KCN</a> → 2 K[Au(CN)<sub>2</sub>] + 2 <a href="/wiki/Potassium_hydroxide" title="Potassium hydroxide">KOH</a>. Zinc is added to the mix and, being more <a href="/wiki/Reactivity_series" title="Reactivity series">reactive</a> than gold, displaces the gold: 2[Au(CN)<sub>2</sub>] + Zn → K<sub>2</sub>[Zn(CN)<sub>4</sub>] + 2 Au. The gold precipitates out of solution as a sludge, and is filtered off and melted.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[155]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Properties_compared_with_light_metals">Properties compared with light metals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metals&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Properties compared with light metals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Some general physical and chemical properties of light and heavy metals are summarised in the table. The comparison should be treated with caution since the terms light metal and heavy metal are not always consistently defined. Also the physical properties of hardness and tensile strength can vary widely depending on purity, <a href="/wiki/Crystallite" title="Crystallite">grain size</a> and pre-treatment.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[156]</a></sup></p>
