</tr>
</table>
<p>These properties make it relatively easy to distinguish a light metal like sodium from a heavy metal like tungsten, but the differences become less clear at the boundaries. Light structural metals like beryllium, scandium, and titanium have some of the characteristics of heavy metals, such as higher melting points;<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">[n 27]</a></sup> post-transition heavy metals like zinc, cadmium, and lead have some of the characteristics of light metals, such as being relatively soft, having lower melting points,<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">[n 28]</a></sup> and forming mainly colourless complexes.<sup id="cite_ref-longo_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-longo-23">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Herron_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Herron-25">[22]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nathans_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nathans-26">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">Uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metals&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Heavy metals are present in nearly all aspects of modern life. Iron may be the most common as it accounts for 90% of all refined metals. Platinum may be the most ubiquitous given it is said to be found in, or used to produce, 20% of all consumer goods.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">[180]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some common uses of heavy metals depend on the general characteristics of metals such as <a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductivity" class="mw-redirect" title="Electrical conductivity">electrical conductivity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reflectivity" class="mw-redirect" title="Reflectivity">reflectivity</a> or the general characteristics of heavy metals such as density, strength, and durability. Other uses depend on the characteristics of the specific element, such as their biological role as nutrients or poisons or some other specific atomic properties. Examples of such atomic properties include: partly filled <a href="/wiki/D-orbital" class="mw-redirect" title="D-orbital">d-</a> or <a href="/wiki/F-orbital" class="mw-redirect" title="F-orbital">f- orbitals</a> (in many of the transition, lanthanide, and actinide heavy metals) that enable the formation of coloured compounds;<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">[181]</a></sup> the capacity of most heavy metal ions (such as platinum,<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">[182]</a></sup> cerium<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">[183]</a></sup> or bismuth<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">[184]</a></sup>) to exist in different <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation states</a> and therefore act as catalysts;<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">[185]</a></sup> poorly overlapping 3d or 4f orbitals (in iron, cobalt, and nickel, or the lanthanide heavy metals from <a href="/wiki/Europium" title="Europium">europium</a> through <a href="/wiki/Thulium" title="Thulium">thulium</a>) that give rise to magnetic effects;<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">[186]</a></sup> and high atomic numbers and <a href="/wiki/Electron_density" title="Electron density">electron densities</a> that underpin their nuclear science applications.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">[187]</a></sup> Typical uses of heavy metals can be broadly grouped into the following six categories.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">[188]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">[n 29]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Weight-_or_density-based">Weight- or density-based</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metals&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Weight- or density-based">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CelloCloseup1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Looking down on the top of a small wooden boat-like shape. Four metal strings run along the middle of the shape down its long axis. The strings pass over a small raised wooden bridge positioned in the centre of the shape so that the strings sit above the deck of the cello." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/CelloCloseup1.jpg/220px-CelloCloseup1.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/CelloCloseup1.jpg/330px-CelloCloseup1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/CelloCloseup1.jpg/440px-CelloCloseup1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="676" data-file-height="507" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CelloCloseup1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
In a <a href="/wiki/Cello" title="Cello">cello</a> (example shown above) or a <a href="/wiki/Viola" title="Viola">viola</a> the C-<a href="/wiki/String_(music)" title="String (music)">string</a> sometimes incorporates <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a>; its high density permits a smaller diameter string and improves responsiveness.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">[189]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Some uses of heavy metals, including in sport, <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_engineering" title="Mechanical engineering">mechanical engineering</a>, <a href="/wiki/Artillery" title="Artillery">military ordnance</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_science" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear science">nuclear science</a>, take advantage of their relatively high densities. In <a href="/wiki/Underwater_diving" title="Underwater diving">underwater diving</a>, lead is used as a <a href="/wiki/Ballast" title="Ballast">ballast</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-Emsley_2011_286_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley_2011_286-219">[190]</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/Handicapping#Horse_racing" title="Handicapping">handicap horse racing</a> each horse must carry a specified lead weight, based on factors including past performance, so as to equalize the chances of the various competitors.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">[191]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Golf" title="Golf">golf</a>, tungsten, <a href="/wiki/Brass" title="Brass">brass</a>, or copper inserts in <a href="/wiki/Fairway_(golf)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fairway (golf)">fairway</a> <a href="/wiki/Golf_club" title="Golf club">clubs and irons</a> lower the centre of gravity of the club making it easier to get the ball into the air;<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">[192]</a></sup> and golf balls with tungsten cores are claimed to have better flight characteristics.<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">[193]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Fly_fishing" title="Fly fishing">fly fishing</a>, sinking fly lines have a <a href="/wiki/Polyvinyl_chloride" title="Polyvinyl chloride">PVC</a> coating embedded with tungsten powder, so that they sink at the required rate.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">[194]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Track_and_field" title="Track and field">track and field</a> sport, <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a> balls used in the <a href="/wiki/Hammer_throw" title="Hammer throw">hammer throw</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shot_put" title="Shot put">shot put</a> events are filled with lead in order to attain the minimum weight required under international rules.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">[195]</a></sup> Tungsten was used in hammer throw balls at least up to 1980; the minimum size of the ball was increased in 1981 to eliminate the need for what was, at that time, an expensive metal (triple the cost of other hammers) not generally available in all countries.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">[196]</a></sup> Tungsten hammers were so dense that they penetrated too deeply into the turf.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">[197]</a></sup></p>
<p>In mechanical engineering, heavy metals are used for ballast in boats,<sup id="cite_ref-Moore_1984_102_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moore_1984_102-227">[198]</a></sup> aeroplanes,<sup id="cite_ref-NMAB1973_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NMAB1973-228">[199]</a></sup> and motor vehicles;<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">[200]</a></sup> or in <a href="/wiki/Tire_balance" title="Tire balance">balance weights on wheels</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crankshaft" title="Crankshaft">crankshafts</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">[201]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gyroscope" title="Gyroscope">gyroscopes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Propeller" title="Propeller">propellers</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">[202]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Centrifugal_clutch" title="Centrifugal clutch">centrifugal clutches</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">[203]</a></sup> in situations requiring maximum weight in minimum space (for example in <a href="/wiki/Movement_(clockwork)" title="Movement (clockwork)">watch movements</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-NMAB1973_228-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NMAB1973-228">[199]</a></sup></p>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: left; clear: left; margin: 0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0; width:20%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: cornsilk;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>The higher the projectile density, the more effectively it can penetrate heavy armor plate&#160;... <a href="/wiki/Osmium" title="Osmium">Os</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iridium" title="Iridium">Ir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">Pt</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rhenium" title="Rhenium">Re</a>&#160;... are expensive&#160;... <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">U</a> offers an appealing combination of high density, reasonable cost and high fracture toughness.</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">AM Russell and KL Lee<br />
<i>Structure–property relations<br />
in nonferrous metals</i> (2005, p. 16)</cite></div>
<p>In military ordnance, tungsten or uranium is used in <a href="/wiki/Chobham_armour#Heavy_metal_modules" title="Chobham armour">armour plating</a><sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">[204]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy_penetrator" title="Kinetic energy penetrator">armour piercing projectiles</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">[205]</a></sup> as well as in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon_design" title="Nuclear weapon design">nuclear weapons</a> to increase efficiency (by <a href="/wiki/Neutron_reflector" title="Neutron reflector">reflecting neutrons</a> and momentarily delaying the expansion of reacting materials).<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">[206]</a></sup> In the 1970s, <a href="/wiki/Tantalum" title="Tantalum">tantalum</a> was found to be more effective than copper in <a href="/wiki/Shaped_charge" title="Shaped charge">shaped charge</a> and <a href="/wiki/Explosively_formed_penetrator" title="Explosively formed penetrator">explosively formed anti-armour weapons</a> on account of its higher density, allowing greater force concentration, and better deformability.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">[207]</a></sup> Less-<a href="/wiki/Toxic_heavy_metal" title="Toxic heavy metal">toxic heavy metals</a>, such as copper, tin, tungsten, and bismuth, and probably manganese (as well as <a href="/wiki/Boron" title="Boron">boron</a>, a metalloid), have replaced lead and antimony in the <a href="/wiki/Green_bullets" class="mw-redirect" title="Green bullets">green bullets</a> used by some armies and in some recreational shooting munitions.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">[208]</a></sup> Doubts have been raised about the safety (or <a href="/wiki/Environmentally_friendly" title="Environmentally friendly">green credentials</a>) of tungsten.<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">[209]</a></sup></p>
<p>Because denser materials absorb more radioactive emissions than lighter ones, heavy metals are useful for <a href="/wiki/Radiation_protection" title="Radiation protection">radiation shielding</a> and to <a href="/wiki/Collimator#Neutron.2C_X-ray.2C_and_gamma_ray_collimators" title="Collimator">focus radiation beams</a> in <a href="/wiki/Linear_accelerator" class="mw-redirect" title="Linear accelerator">linear accelerators</a> and <a href="/wiki/Radiotherapy" class="mw-redirect" title="Radiotherapy">radiotherapy</a> applications.<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">[210]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Strength-_or_durability-based">Strength- or durability-based</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metals&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Strength- or durability-based">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Statue-de-la-liberte-new-york.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A colossal statue of a robed female figure who bears a torch in her raised left hand and a tablet in her other hand" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Statue-de-la-liberte-new-york.jpg/220px-Statue-de-la-liberte-new-york.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Statue-de-la-liberte-new-york.jpg/330px-Statue-de-la-liberte-new-york.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Statue-de-la-liberte-new-york.jpg/440px-Statue-de-la-liberte-new-york.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1704" data-file-height="2272" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Statue-de-la-liberte-new-york.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Statue_of_Liberty" title="Statue of Liberty">Statue of Liberty</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Stainless_steel" title="Stainless steel">stainless steel</a> alloy<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">[211]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Armature_(sculpture)" title="Armature (sculpture)">armature</a> provides structural strength; a <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> skin confers corrosion resistance.<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">[n 30]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The strength or durability of heavy metals such as chromium, iron, nickel, copper, zinc, molybdenum, tin, tungsten, and lead, as well as their alloys, makes them useful for the manufacture of artefacts such as tools, machinery,<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">[213]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Domestic_appliances" class="mw-redirect" title="Domestic appliances">appliances</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Bradl_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bradl-244">[214]</a></sup> utensils,<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">[215]</a></sup> pipes,<sup id="cite_ref-Bradl_244-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bradl-244">[214]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Railroad_track" class="mw-redirect" title="Railroad track">railroad tracks</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">[216]</a></sup> buildings<sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">[217]</a></sup> and bridges,<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">[218]</a></sup> automobiles,<sup id="cite_ref-Bradl_244-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bradl-244">[214]</a></sup> locks,<sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">[219]</a></sup> furniture,<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">[220]</a></sup> ships,<sup id="cite_ref-Moore_1984_102_227-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moore_1984_102-227">[198]</a></sup> planes,<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">[221]</a></sup> coinage<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">[222]</a></sup> and jewellery.<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">[223]</a></sup> They are also used as alloying additives for enhancing the properties of other metals.<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">[n 31]</a></sup> Of the two dozen elements that have been used in the world's monetised coinage only two, carbon and aluminium, are not heavy metals.<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">[225]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">[n 32]</a></sup> Gold, silver, and platinum are used in jewellery<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">[n 33]</a></sup> as are (for example) nickel, copper, indium, and cobalt in <a href="/wiki/Colored_gold" title="Colored gold">coloured gold</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">[228]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Costume_jewelry" title="Costume jewelry">Low-cost jewellery</a> and <a href="/wiki/Toy#Safety_regulations" title="Toy">children's toys</a> may be made, to a significant degree, of heavy metals such as chromium, nickel, cadmium, or lead.<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">[229]</a></sup></p>
<p>Copper, zinc, tin, and lead are mechanically weaker metals but have useful <a href="/wiki/Corrosion" title="Corrosion">corrosion</a> prevention properties. While each of them will react with air, the resulting <a href="/wiki/Patina" title="Patina">patinas</a> of either various copper salts,<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">[230]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zinc_carbonate" class="mw-redirect" title="Zinc carbonate">zinc carbonate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tin_dioxide" title="Tin dioxide">tin oxide</a>, or a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Lead(II)_oxide" title="Lead(II) oxide">lead oxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lead_carbonate" title="Lead carbonate">carbonate</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lead_sulfate" class="mw-redirect" title="Lead sulfate">sulfate</a>, confer valuable <a href="/wiki/Passivation_(chemistry)" title="Passivation (chemistry)">protective properties</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">[231]</a></sup> Copper and lead are therefore used, for example, as <a href="/wiki/Roofing_material" class="mw-redirect" title="Roofing material">roofing materials</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">[232]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">[n 34]</a></sup> zinc acts as an <a href="/wiki/Anti-corrosion" title="Anti-corrosion">anti-corrosion</a> agent in <a href="/wiki/Galvanised_steel" class="mw-redirect" title="Galvanised steel">galvanised steel</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">[233]</a></sup> and tin serves a similar purpose on <a href="/wiki/Steel_can" class="mw-redirect" title="Steel can">steel cans</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">[234]</a></sup></p>
<p>The workability and corrosion resistance of iron and chromium are increased by adding <a href="/wiki/Gadolinium" title="Gadolinium">gadolinium</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Creep_(deformation)" title="Creep (deformation)">creep resistance</a> of nickel is improved with the addition of thorium. Tellurium is added to copper and steel alloys to improve their machinability; and to lead to make it harder and more acid-resistant.<sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">[235]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_and_chemical">Biological and chemical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metals&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Biological and chemical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cerium(IV)_oxide.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A small colorless saucer holding a pale-yellow powder" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Cerium%28IV%29_oxide.jpg/220px-Cerium%28IV%29_oxide.jpg" width="220" height="223" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Cerium%28IV%29_oxide.jpg/330px-Cerium%28IV%29_oxide.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Cerium%28IV%29_oxide.jpg/440px-Cerium%28IV%29_oxide.jpg 2x" data-file-width="597" data-file-height="605" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cerium(IV)_oxide.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Ceria" class="mw-redirect" title="Ceria">Cerium(IV) oxide</a> (sample shown above) is used as a <a href="/wiki/Catalyst" class="mw-redirect" title="Catalyst">catalyst</a> in <a href="/wiki/Self-cleaning_oven" title="Self-cleaning oven">self-cleaning ovens</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270">[236]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Biocide" title="Biocide">biocidal</a> effects of <a href="/wiki/Oligodynamic_effect" title="Oligodynamic effect">some heavy metals</a> have been known since antiquity.<sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">[237]</a></sup> Platinum, osmium, copper, ruthenium, and other heavy metals, including arsenic, are used in anti-cancer treatments, or have shown potential.<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272">[238]</a></sup> Antimony (anti-protozoal), bismuth (<a href="/wiki/Antiulcer_agent" class="mw-redirect" title="Antiulcer agent">anti-ulcer</a>), gold (<a href="/wiki/Arthritis#Medications" title="Arthritis">anti-arthritic</a>), and iron (<a href="/wiki/Anti-malarial_medication" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-malarial medication">anti-malarial</a>) are also important in medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273">[239]</a></sup> Copper, zinc, silver, gold, or mercury are used in <a href="/wiki/Antiseptic" title="Antiseptic">antiseptic</a> formulations;<sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274">[240]</a></sup> small amounts of some heavy metals are used to control algal growth in, for example, <a href="/wiki/Cooling_tower" title="Cooling tower">cooling towers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">[241]</a></sup> Depending on their intended use as fertilisers or biocides, <a href="/wiki/Agrochemical" title="Agrochemical">agrochemicals</a> may contain heavy metals such as chromium, cobalt, nickel, copper, zinc, arsenic, cadmium, mercury, or lead.<sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276">[242]</a></sup></p>
<p>Selected heavy metals are used as catalysts in fuel processing (rhenium, for example), synthetic rubber and fibre production (bismuth), <a href="/wiki/Catalytic_converter#Construction" title="Catalytic converter">emission control devices</a> (palladium), and in <a href="/wiki/Self-cleaning_oven" title="Self-cleaning oven">self-cleaning ovens</a> (where <a href="/wiki/Cerium_oxide" title="Cerium oxide">cerium(IV) oxide</a> in the walls of such ovens helps <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">oxidise</a> <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>-based cooking residues).<sup id="cite_ref-277" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-277">[243]</a></sup> In soap chemistry, heavy metals form insoluble soaps that are used in <a href="/wiki/Grease_(lubricant)" title="Grease (lubricant)">lubricating greases</a>, paint dryers, and <a href="/wiki/Fungicide" title="Fungicide">fungicides</a> (apart from lithium, the alkali metals and the <a href="/wiki/Ammonium" title="Ammonium">ammonium</a> ion form soluble soaps).<sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-278">[244]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colouring_and_optics">Colouring and optics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metals&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Colouring and optics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Neodym(III)sulfat.JPG" class="image"><img alt="Small translucent, pink-coloured crystals a bit like the colour of candy floss" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Neodym%28III%29sulfat.JPG/220px-Neodym%28III%29sulfat.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Neodym%28III%29sulfat.JPG/330px-Neodym%28III%29sulfat.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Neodym%28III%29sulfat.JPG/440px-Neodym%28III%29sulfat.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2312" data-file-height="1738" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Neodym(III)sulfat.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Neodymium sulfate (Nd<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>), used to colour glassware<sup id="cite_ref-279" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-279">[245]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The colours of <a href="/wiki/Glass" title="Glass">glass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ceramic_glazes" class="mw-redirect" title="Ceramic glazes">ceramic glazes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_paint" title="Environmental impact of paint">paints</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pigment#Metal-based_pigments" title="Pigment">pigments</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Plastic" title="Plastic">plastics</a> are commonly produced by the inclusion of heavy metals (or their compounds) such as chromium, manganese, cobalt, copper, zinc, selenium, <a href="/wiki/Zirconium" title="Zirconium">zirconium</a>, molybdenum, silver, tin, <a href="/wiki/Praseodymium" title="Praseodymium">praseodymium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neodymium" title="Neodymium">neodymium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Erbium" title="Erbium">erbium</a>, tungsten, iridium, gold, lead, or uranium.<sup id="cite_ref-280" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-280">[246]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tattoo" title="Tattoo">Tattoo</a> inks may contain heavy metals, such as chromium, cobalt, nickel, and copper.<sup id="cite_ref-281" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-281">[247]</a></sup> The high reflectivity of some heavy metals is important in the construction of <a href="/wiki/Mirror" title="Mirror">mirrors</a>, including precision <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_instrument" class="mw-redirect" title="Astronomical instrument">astronomical instruments</a>. Headlight reflectors rely on the excellent reflectivity of a thin film of rhodium.<sup id="cite_ref-282" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-282">[248]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electronics.2C_magnets.2C_and_lighting">Electronics, magnets, and lighting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metals&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Electronics, magnets, and lighting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Topaz_Solar_Farm,_California_Valley.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A satellite image of what look like semi-regularly spaced swathes of black tiles set in a plain, surrounded by farmland and grass lands" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Topaz_Solar_Farm%2C_California_Valley.jpg/220px-Topaz_Solar_Farm%2C_California_Valley.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Topaz_Solar_Farm%2C_California_Valley.jpg/330px-Topaz_Solar_Farm%2C_California_Valley.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Topaz_Solar_Farm%2C_California_Valley.jpg/440px-Topaz_Solar_Farm%2C_California_Valley.jpg 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="480" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Topaz_Solar_Farm,_California_Valley.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Topaz_Solar_Farm" title="Topaz Solar Farm">Topaz Solar Farm</a>, in southern California, features 9 million <a href="/wiki/Cadmium_telluride_photovoltaics" title="Cadmium telluride photovoltaics">cadmium-tellurium photovoltaic modules</a> covering an area of 25.6 square kilometres (9.5 square miles).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Heavy metals or their compounds can be found in <a href="/wiki/Electronic_component" title="Electronic component">electronic components</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electrode" title="Electrode">electrodes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Electrical_wiring" title="Electrical wiring">wiring</a> and <a href="/wiki/Solar_panel" title="Solar panel">solar panels</a> where they may be used as either conductors, semiconductors, or insulators. Molybdenum powder is used in <a href="/wiki/Circuit_board" class="mw-redirect" title="Circuit board">circuit board</a> inks.<sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283">[249]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ruthenium(IV)_oxide" title="Ruthenium(IV) oxide">Ruthenium(IV) oxide</a> coated titanium <a href="/wiki/Anode" title="Anode">anodes</a> are used for the industrial production of <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284">[250]</a></sup> Home electrical systems, for the most part, are wired with copper wire for its good conducting properties.<sup id="cite_ref-285" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-285">[251]</a></sup> Silver and gold are used in electrical and electronic devices, particularly in contact <a href="/wiki/Switch" title="Switch">switches</a>, as a result of their high electrical conductivity and capacity to resist or minimise the formation of impurities on their surfaces.<sup id="cite_ref-286" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-286">[252]</a></sup> The semiconductors <a href="/wiki/Cadmium_telluride" title="Cadmium telluride">cadmium telluride</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gallium_arsenide" title="Gallium arsenide">gallium arsenide</a> are used to make solar panels. <a href="/wiki/Hafnium_oxide" class="mw-redirect" title="Hafnium oxide">Hafnium oxide</a>, an insulator, is used as a <a href="/wiki/Voltage_controller" title="Voltage controller">voltage controller</a> in <a href="/wiki/Microchip" class="mw-redirect" title="Microchip">microchips</a>; <a href="/wiki/Tantalum_oxide" class="mw-redirect" title="Tantalum oxide">tantalum oxide</a>, another insulator, is used in <a href="/wiki/Capacitor" title="Capacitor">capacitors</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mobile_phone" title="Mobile phone">mobile phones</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-287" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-287">[253]</a></sup> Heavy metals have been used in batteries for over 200 years, at least since <a href="/wiki/Alessandro_Volta" title="Alessandro Volta">Volta</a> invented his copper and silver <a href="/wiki/Voltaic_pile" title="Voltaic pile">voltaic pile</a> in 1800.<sup id="cite_ref-288" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-288">[254]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Promethium" title="Promethium">Promethium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lanthanum" title="Lanthanum">lanthanum</a>, and mercury are further examples found in, respectively, <a href="/wiki/Atomic_battery" title="Atomic battery">atomic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nickel-metal_hydride_battery" class="mw-redirect" title="Nickel-metal hydride battery">nickel-metal hydride</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Button_cell" title="Button cell">button cell</a> batteries.<sup id="cite_ref-289" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-289">[255]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Magnet" title="Magnet">Magnets</a> are made of heavy metals such as manganese, iron, cobalt, nickel, niobium, bismuth, praseodymium, neodymium, gadolinium, and <a href="/wiki/Dysprosium" title="Dysprosium">dysprosium</a>. Neodymium magnets are the strongest type of <a href="/wiki/Magnet#Types_of_permanent_magnets" title="Magnet">permanent magnet</a> commercially available. They are key components of, for example, car door locks, <a href="/wiki/Starter_motor" class="mw-redirect" title="Starter motor">starter motors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fuel_pump" title="Fuel pump">fuel pumps</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Power_window" title="Power window">power windows</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-290" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-290">[256]</a></sup></p>
<p>Heavy metals are used in <a href="/wiki/Lighting" title="Lighting">lighting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">lasers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Light-emitting_diode" title="Light-emitting diode">light-emitting diodes</a> (LEDs). <a href="/wiki/Flat_panel_display" title="Flat panel display">Flat panel displays</a> incorporate a thin film of electrically conducting <a href="/wiki/Indium_tin_oxide" title="Indium tin oxide">indium tin oxide</a>. <a href="/wiki/Fluorescent_lighting" class="mw-redirect" title="Fluorescent lighting">Fluorescent lighting</a> relies on mercury vapour for its operation. <a href="/wiki/Ruby_laser" title="Ruby laser">Ruby lasers</a> generate deep red beams by exciting chromium atoms; the lanthanides are also extensively employed in lasers. Gallium, indium, and arsenic;<sup id="cite_ref-291" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-291">[257]</a></sup> and copper, iridium, and platinum are used in LEDs (the latter three in <a href="/wiki/OLED" title="OLED">organic LEDs</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-292" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-292">[258]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear">Nuclear</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metals&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Nuclear">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rotating_anode_x-ray_tube_(labeled).jpg" class="image"><img alt="A large glass bulb. Inside the bulb, at one end, is a fixed spindle. There is an arm attached to the spindle. At the end of the arm is a small protuberance. This is the cathode. At the other end of the bulb is a rotatable wide metal plate attached to a rotor mechanism which protrudes from the end of the bulb." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Rotating_anode_x-ray_tube_%28labeled%29.jpg/250px-Rotating_anode_x-ray_tube_%28labeled%29.jpg" width="250" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Rotating_anode_x-ray_tube_%28labeled%29.jpg/375px-Rotating_anode_x-ray_tube_%28labeled%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Rotating_anode_x-ray_tube_%28labeled%29.jpg/500px-Rotating_anode_x-ray_tube_%28labeled%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="451" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rotating_anode_x-ray_tube_(labeled).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An <a href="/wiki/X-ray_tube" title="X-ray tube">X-ray tube</a> with a rotating anode, typically a <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a>-<a href="/wiki/Rhenium" title="Rhenium">rhenium</a> alloy on a <a href="/wiki/Molybdenum" title="Molybdenum">molybdenum</a> core, backed with <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">graphite</a><sup id="cite_ref-Ball_293-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ball-293">[259]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-296" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-296">[n 35]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Niche uses of heavy metals with high atomic numbers occur in <a href="/wiki/Diagnostic_imaging" class="mw-redirect" title="Diagnostic imaging">diagnostic imaging</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transmission_electron_microscopy" title="Transmission electron microscopy">electron microscopy</a>, and nuclear science. In diagnostic imaging, heavy metals such as cobalt or tungsten make up the anode materials found in <a href="/wiki/X-ray_tube" title="X-ray tube">x-ray tubes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Tisza_297-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tisza-297">[262]</a></sup> In electron microscopy, heavy metals such as lead, gold, palladium, platinum, or uranium are used to make conductive coatings and to introduce electron density into biological specimens by <a href="/wiki/Staining" title="Staining">staining</a>, <a href="/wiki/Negative_staining" class="mw-redirect" title="Negative staining">negative staining</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Evaporation_(deposition)" title="Evaporation (deposition)">vacuum deposition</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-298" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-298">[263]</a></sup> In nuclear science, nuclei of heavy metals such as chromium, iron, or zinc are sometimes fired at other heavy metal targets to produce <a href="/wiki/Transuranium_element#Super-heavy_elements" title="Transuranium element">superheavy elements</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-299" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-299">[264]</a></sup> heavy metals are also employed as <a href="/wiki/Spallation#Nuclear_spallation" title="Spallation">spallation</a> targets for the production of <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a><sup id="cite_ref-300" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-300">[265]</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope" class="mw-redirect" title="Radioisotope">radioisotopes</a> such as astatine (using lead, bismuth, thorium, or uranium in the latter case).<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_301-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-301">[266]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metals&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-15">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Criteria used were <i>density:</i><sup id="cite_ref-Duffus798_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Duffus798-2">[2]</a></sup> (1) above 3.5&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup>; (2) above 7&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup>; <i>atomic weight:</i> (3) &gt; 22.98;<sup id="cite_ref-Duffus798_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Duffus798-2">[2]</a></sup> (4) &gt; 40 (excluding <a href="/wiki/S-block" class="mw-redirect" title="S-block">s-</a> and <a href="/wiki/F-block" class="mw-redirect" title="F-block">f-block</a> metals);<sup id="cite_ref-Rand_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rand-3">[3]</a></sup> (5) &gt; 200;<sup id="cite_ref-Baldwin_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baldwin-4">[4]</a></sup> <i>atomic number:</i> (6) &gt; 20; (7) 21–92;<sup id="cite_ref-Lyman_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lyman-5">[5]</a></sup> <i>chemical behaviour:</i> (8) United States Pharmacopeia;<sup id="cite_ref-USP_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USP-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Raghuram_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Raghuram-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Thorne_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thorne-8">[8]</a></sup> (9) Hawkes' periodic table-based definition (excluding the <a href="/wiki/Lanthanide" title="Lanthanide">lanthanides</a> and <a href="/wiki/Actinide" title="Actinide">actinides</a>);<sup id="cite_ref-Hawkes_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hawkes-9">[9]</a></sup> and (10) Nieboer and Richardson's biochemical classifications.<sup id="cite_ref-Nieboer_1980_4_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nieboer_1980_4-10">[10]</a></sup> Densities of the elements are mainly from Emsley.<sup id="cite_ref-Emsley_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley-11">[11]</a></sup> Predicted densities have been used for <a href="/wiki/Astatine" title="Astatine">At</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francium" title="Francium">Fr</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fermium" title="Fermium">Fm</a>–<a href="/wiki/Tennessine" title="Tennessine">Ts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-HoffBon_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HoffBon-12">[12]</a></sup> Indicative densities were derived for <a href="/wiki/Fermium" title="Fermium">Fm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mendelevium" title="Mendelevium">Md</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nobelium" title="Nobelium">No</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lawrencium" title="Lawrencium">Lr</a> based on their atomic weights, estimated <a href="/wiki/Metallic_radius" class="mw-redirect" title="Metallic radius">metallic radii</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> and predicted <a href="/wiki/Close-packing_of_equal_spheres" title="Close-packing of equal spheres">close-packed</a> crystalline structures.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Atomic weights are from Emsley.,<sup id="cite_ref-Emsley_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley-11">[11]</a></sup> inside back cover</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-17">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Metalloids were, however, excluded from Hawkes' periodic table-based definition given he noted it was "not necessary to decide whether semimetals [i.e. metalloids] should be included as heavy metals."<sup id="cite_ref-Hawkes_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hawkes-9">[9]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-22">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The test is not specific for any particular metals but is said to be capable of at least detecting <a href="/wiki/Molybdenum" title="Molybdenum">Mo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">Cu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">Ag</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cadmium" title="Cadmium">Cd</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">Hg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">Sn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">Pb</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">As</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antimony" title="Antimony">Sb</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bismuth" title="Bismuth">Bi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Raghuram_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Raghuram-7">[7]</a></sup> In any event, when the test uses <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a> as the reagent cannot detect <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">Th</a>, <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">Ti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zirconium" title="Zirconium">Zr</a>, <a href="/wiki/Niobium" title="Niobium">Nb</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tantalum" title="Tantalum">Ta</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Chromium" title="Chromium">Cr</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Thorne_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thorne-8">[8]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-27"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-27">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Transition and post-transition metals that do not usually form coloured complexes are <a href="/wiki/Scandium" title="Scandium">Sc</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yttrium" title="Yttrium">Y</a> in <a href="/wiki/Group_3_element" title="Group 3 element">group 3</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-longo_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-longo-23">[20]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">Ag</a> in <a href="/wiki/Group_11" class="mw-redirect" title="Group 11">group 11</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[21]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">Zn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cadmium" title="Cadmium">Cd</a> in group 12;<sup id="cite_ref-longo_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-longo-23">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Herron_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Herron-25">[22]</a></sup> and the metals of groups <a href="/wiki/Group_13_element" class="mw-redirect" title="Group 13 element">13</a>–<a href="/wiki/Group_16_element" class="mw-redirect" title="Group 16 element">16</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Nathans_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nathans-26">[23]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-40"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-40">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Lanthanide (Ln) sulfides and hydroxides are insoluble;<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[24]</a></sup> the latter can be obtained from aqueous solutions of Ln salts as coloured gelatinous precipitates;<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[25]</a></sup> and Ln complexes have much the same colour as their aqua ions (the majority of which are coloured).<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[26]</a></sup> Actinide (An) sulfides may or may not be insoluble, depending on the author. Divalent <a href="/wiki/Uranium_monosulfide" title="Uranium monosulfide">uranium monosulfide</a> is not attacked by boiling water.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[27]</a></sup> Trivalent actinide ions behave similarly to the trivalent lanthanide ions hence the sulfides in question may be insoluble but this is not explicitly stated.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[28]</a></sup> Tervalent An sulfides decompose<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[29]</a></sup> but Edelstein et al. say they are soluble<sup id="cite_ref-Edelstein_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edelstein-34">[30]</a></sup> whereas Haynes says <a href="/wiki/Thorium(IV)_sulfide" title="Thorium(IV) sulfide">thorium(IV) sulfide</a> is insoluble.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[31]</a></sup> Early in the history of nuclear fission it had been noted that precipitation with <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a> was a "remarkably" effective way of isolating and detecting <a href="/wiki/Transuranium_element" title="Transuranium element">transuranium elements</a> in solution.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[32]</a></sup> In a similar vein, Deschlag writes that the elements after uranium were expected to have insoluble sulfides by analogy with third row transition metals. But he goes on to note that the elements after <a href="/wiki/Actinium" title="Actinium">actinium</a> were found to have properties different from those of the transition metals and claims they do not form insoluble sulfides.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[33]</a></sup> The An hydroxides are, however, insoluble<sup id="cite_ref-Edelstein_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edelstein-34">[30]</a></sup> and can be precipitated from aqueous solutions of their salts.<sup id="cite_ref-Wulfsberg_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wulfsberg-38">[34]</a></sup> Finally, many An complexes have "deep and vivid" colours.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[35]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-45"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-45">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The heavier elements commonly to less commonly recognised as <a href="/wiki/Metalloids" class="mw-redirect" title="Metalloids">metalloids</a>—<a href="/wiki/Germanium" title="Germanium">Ge</a>; <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">As</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antimony" title="Antimony">Sb</a>; <a href="/wiki/Selenium" title="Selenium">Se</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tellurium" title="Tellurium">Te</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polonium" title="Polonium">Po</a>; <a href="/wiki/Astatine" title="Astatine">At</a>—satisfy some of the three parts of Hawkes' definition. All of them have insoluble sulfides<sup id="cite_ref-Wulfsberg_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wulfsberg-38">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Korenman_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Korenman-41">[36]</a></sup> but only Ge, Te, and Po apparently have effectively insoluble hydroxides.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[37]</a></sup> All bar At can be obtained as coloured (sulfide) precipitates from aqueous solutions of their salts;<sup id="cite_ref-Wulfsberg_38-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wulfsberg-38">[34]</a></sup> astatine is likewise precipitated from solution by hydrogen sulfide but, since visible quantities of At have never been synthesised, the colour of the precipitate is not known.<sup id="cite_ref-Korenman_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Korenman-41">[36]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[38]</a></sup> As <a href="/wiki/Block_(periodic_table)" title="Block (periodic table)">p-block elements</a>, their complexes are usually colourless.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[39]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-48"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-48">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The class A and class B terminology is analogous to the <a href="/wiki/HSAB_theory" title="HSAB theory">"hard acid" and "soft base"</a> terminology sometimes used to refer to the behaviour of metal ions in inorganic systems.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[41]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-51"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-51">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Be and Al are exceptions to this general trend. They have somewhat higher electronegativity values.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[42]</a></sup> Being relatively small their +2 or +3 ions ions have high charge densities, thereby polarising nearby electron clouds. The net result is that Be and Al compounds having considerable covalent character.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[43]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-54"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-54">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Google_Scholar" title="Google Scholar">Google Scholar</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://scholar.google.com.au/scholar?hl=en&amp;q=The+replacement+of+the+nondescript+term+%27heavy+metals%27+by+a+biologically+and+chemically+significant+classification+of+metal+ions%22&amp;btnG=&amp;as_sdt=1%2C5&amp;as_sdtp=">has recorded</a> more than 900 citations for the paper in question.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[45]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-78"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-78">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">If Gmelin had been working with the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_units" title="Imperial units">imperial system</a> of weights and measures he may have chosen 300&#160;lbs/ft<sup>3</sup> as his light/heavy metal cutoff in which case selenium (density 300.27&#160;lbs/ft<sup>3</sup> ) would have made the grade, whereas 5 gm/cm<sup>3</sup> = 312.14lbs/ft<sup>3</sup>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-89"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-89">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Lead, which is a <a href="/wiki/Bioaccumulation" title="Bioaccumulation">cumulative poison</a>, has a relatively high abundance due to its extensive historical use and human-caused discharge into the environment.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[78]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-91"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-91">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Haynes shows an amount of &lt; 17 mg for tin<sup id="cite_ref-Haynes_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haynes-90">[79]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-93"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-93">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Iyengar records a figure of 5 mg for nickel;<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[80]</a></sup> Haynes shows an amount of 10 mg<sup id="cite_ref-Haynes_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haynes-90">[79]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-95"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-95">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Encompassing 45 heavy metals occurring in quantities of less than 10 mg each, including As (7 mg), Mo (5), Co (1.5), and Cr (1.4)<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[81]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-102"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-102">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Of the elements commonly recognised as metalloids, B and Si were counted as nonmetals; Ge, As, Sb, and Te as heavy metals.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-124"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-124">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Ni, Cu, Zn, Se, Ag and Sb appear in the United States Government's <i>Toxic Pollutant List;</i><sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[107]</a></sup> Mn, Co, and Sn are listed in the Australian Government's <i>National Pollutant Inventory.</i><sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[108]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-126"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-126">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Tungsten could be another such toxic heavy metal.<sup id="cite_ref-United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency_2014_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency_2014-125">[109]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-131"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-131">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Selenium is the most toxic of the heavy metals that are essential for mammals.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[113]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-146"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-146">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Trace elements having an abundance <i>much less</i> than the one part per trillion of <a href="/wiki/Radium" title="Radium">Ra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Protactinium" title="Protactinium">Pa</a> (namely <a href="/wiki/Technetium" title="Technetium">Tc</a>, <a href="/wiki/Promethium" title="Promethium">Pm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polonium" title="Polonium">Po</a>, <a href="/wiki/Astatine" title="Astatine">At</a>, <a href="/wiki/Actinium" title="Actinium">Ac</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neptunium" title="Neptunium">Np</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">Pu</a>) are not shown. Abundances are from Lide<sup id="cite_ref-Lide_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lide-143">[125]</a></sup> and Emsley;<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[126]</a></sup> occurrence types are from McQueen.<sup id="cite_ref-McQueen_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McQueen-145">[127]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-152"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-152">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In some cases, for example in the presence of <a href="/wiki/Photodisintegration" title="Photodisintegration">high energy gamma rays</a> or in a <a href="/wiki/Rp-process" title="Rp-process">very high temperature hydrogen rich environment</a>, the subject nuclei may experience neutron loss or proton gain resulting in the production of (comparatively rare) <a href="/wiki/P-nuclei" title="P-nuclei">neutron deficient isotopes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[132]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-156"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-156">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The ejection of matter when two neutron stars collide is attributed to the interaction of their <a href="/wiki/Tidal_force" title="Tidal force">tidal forces</a>, possible crustal disruption, and shock heating (which is what happens if you floor the accelerator in car when the engine is cold).<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[135]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-164"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-164">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Iron, cobalt, nickel, germanium and tin are also siderophiles from a whole of Earth perspective.<sup id="cite_ref-McQueen_145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McQueen-145">[127]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-169"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-169">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Heat escaping from the inner solid core is believed to generate motion in the outer core, which is made of liquid iron alloys. The motion of this liquid generates electrical currents which give rise to a magnetic field.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[146]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-173"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-173">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Heavy metals that occur naturally in quantities too small to be economically mined (Tc, Pm, Po, At, Ac, Np and Pu) are instead produced by <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_transmutation" title="Nuclear transmutation">artificial transmutation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[148]</a></sup> The latter method is also used to produce heavy metals from americium onwards.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[149]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-192"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-192">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Sulfides of the Group 1 and 2 metals, and aluminium, are hydrolysed by water;<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[164]</a></sup> scandium,<sup id="cite_ref-Schweitzer_2010_230_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schweitzer_2010_230-189">[165]</a></sup> yttrium<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">[166]</a></sup> and titanium sulfides<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[167]</a></sup> are insoluble.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-197"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-197">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For example, the hydroxides of <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rubidium" title="Rubidium">rubidium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Caesium" title="Caesium">caesium</a> have solubilities exceeding 100 grams per 100 grams of water<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">[169]</a></sup> whereas those of aluminium (0.0001)<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">[170]</a></sup> and scandium (&lt;0.000 000 15 grams)<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">[171]</a></sup> are regarded as being insoluble.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-203"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-203">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Beryllium has what is described as a "high" melting point of 1560 K; scandium and titanium melt at 1814 and 1941 K.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">[176]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-207"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-207">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Zinc is a soft metal with a <a href="/wiki/Mohs_scale_of_mineral_hardness" title="Mohs scale of mineral hardness">Moh's hardness</a> of 2.5;<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">[177]</a></sup> cadmium and lead have lower hardness ratings of 2.0 and 1.5.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">[178]</a></sup> Zinc has a "low" melting point of 693 K; cadmium and lead melt at 595 and 601 K.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">[179]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-217"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-217">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Some violence and abstraction of detail was applied to the sorting scheme in order to keep the number of categories to a manageable level.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-242"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-242">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The skin has largely turned green due to the formation of a protective <a href="/wiki/Patina" title="Patina">patina</a> composed of <a href="/wiki/Antlerite" title="Antlerite">antlerite</a> Cu<sub>3</sub>(OH)<sub>4</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>, <a href="/wiki/Atacamite" title="Atacamite">atacamite</a> Cu<sub>4</sub>(OH)<sub>6</sub>Cl<sub>2</sub>, <a href="/wiki/Brochantite" title="Brochantite">brochantite</a> Cu<sub>4</sub>(OH)<sub>6</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>, <a href="/wiki/Cuprous_oxide" class="mw-redirect" title="Cuprous oxide">cuprous oxide</a> Cu<sub>2</sub>O, and <a href="/wiki/Tenorite" title="Tenorite">tenorite</a> CuO.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">[212]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-255"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-255">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For the lanthanides, this is their only structural use as they are otherwise too reactive, relatively expensive, and moderately strong at best.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">[224]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-258"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-258">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Weller<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">[226]</a></sup> classifies <a href="/wiki/Coinage_metal" class="mw-redirect" title="Coinage metal">coinage metals</a> as <a href="/wiki/Precious_metal" title="Precious metal">precious metals</a> (e.g., silver, gold, platinum); heavy metals of very high durability (nickel); heavy metals of low durability (copper, iron, zinc, tin, and lead); and <a href="/wiki/Light_metal" title="Light metal">light metals</a> (aluminium).</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-260"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-260">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Emsley<sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">[227]</a></sup> estimates a global loss of six tonnes of gold a year due to 18-carat wedding rings slowly wearing away.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-266"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-266">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Sheet lead exposed to the rigours of industrial and coastal climates will last for centuries<sup id="cite_ref-Emsley_2011_286_219-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley_2011_286-219">[190]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-296"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-296">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Electrons impacting the tungsten anode generate X-rays;<sup id="cite_ref-294" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-294">[260]</a></sup> rhenium gives tungsten better resistance to thermal shock;<sup id="cite_ref-295" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-295">[261]</a></sup> molybdenum and graphite act as heat sinks. Molybdenum also has a density nearly half that of tungsten thereby reducing the weight of the anode.<sup id="cite_ref-Ball_293-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ball-293">[259]</a></sup></span></li>
</ol>
</div>

<li><span id="CITEREFVenugopalLuckey1978"></span>Venugopal B. &amp; Luckey T. D. 1978, <i>Metal Toxicity in Mammals</i>, vol. 2, Plenum Press, New York, <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780306371776" class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn">ISBN 978-0-306-37177-6</a>.</li>
<li><span id="CITEREFVernon2013"></span>Vernon R. E. 2013, "Which elements are metalloids", <i>Journal of Chemical Education</i>, vol. 90, no. 12, pp.&#160;1703–1707, <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1021%2Fed3008457">10.1021/ed3008457</a>.</li>
<li><span id="CITEREFVolesky1990"></span>Volesky B. 1990, <i>Biosorption of Heavy Metals</i>, CRC Press, Boca Raton, <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780849349171" class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn">ISBN 978-0-8493-4917-1</a>.</li>
<li><span id="CITEREFvon_Gleich2006"></span>von Gleich A. 2013, "Outlines of a sustainable metals industry", in A. von Gleich, R. U. Ayres &amp; S. Gößling-Reisemann (eds), <i>Sustainable Metals Management</i>, Springer, Dordrecht, pp.&#160;3–40, <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781402040078" class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn">ISBN 978-1-4020-4007-8</a>.</li>
<li><span id="CITEREFvon_Zeerleder1949"></span>von Zeerleder A. 1949, <i>Technology of Light Metals</i>, Elsevier Publishing Company, New York.</li>
<li><span id="CITEREFWarth1956"></span>Warth A. H. 1956, <i>The Chemistry and Technology of Waxes</i>, Reinhold Publishing Corporation, New York.</li>
<li><span id="CITEREFWeart1983"></span><a href="/wiki/Spencer_R._Weart" title="Spencer R. Weart">Weart S. R.</a> 1983, "The discovery of nuclear fission and a nuclear physics paradigm", in W. Shea (ed.), <i>Otto Hahn and the Rise of Nuclear Physics</i>, <a href="/wiki/D._Reidel" title="D. Reidel">D. Reidel</a> Publishing Company, Dordrecht, pp.&#160;91–133, <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9789027715845" class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn">ISBN 978-90-277-1584-5</a>.</li>
<li><span id="CITEREFWeberRutula2001"></span>Weber D. J. &amp; Rutula W. A. 2001, "Use of metals as microbicides in preventing infections in healthcare", in <i>Disinfection, Sterilization, and Preservation</i>, 5th ed., S. S. Block (ed.), <a href="/wiki/Lippincott,_Williams_%26_Wilkins" class="mw-redirect" title="Lippincott, Williams &amp; Wilkins">Lippincott, Williams &amp; Wilkins</a>, Philadelphia, <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780683307405" class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn">ISBN 978-0-683-30740-5</a>.</li>
<li><span id="CITEREFWeller1976"></span>Weller G. 1976, <i>Cleaning and Preservation of Coins and Medals</i>, S. J. Durst, New York, <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780915262038" class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn">ISBN 978-0-915262-03-8</a>.</li>
<li><span id="CITEREFWhite2010"></span>White C. 2010, <i>Projectile Dynamics in Sport: Principles and Applications</i>, <a href="/wiki/Routledge" title="Routledge">Routledge</a>, London, <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780415473316" class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn">ISBN 978-0-415-47331-6</a>.</li>
<li><span id="CITEREFWiberg2001"></span>Wiberg N. 2001, <i>Inorganic Chemistry</i>, Academic Press, San Diego, <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780123526519" class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn">ISBN 978-0-12-352651-9</a>.</li>
<li><span id="CITEREFWijayawardenaMegharajNaidu2016"></span>Wijayawardena M. A. A., Megharaj M. &amp; Naidu R. 2016, "Exposure, toxicity, health impacts and bioavailability of heavy metal mixtures", in D. L. Sparks, <i>Advances in Agronomy</i>, vol. 138, pp.&#160;175–234, Academic Press, London, <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780128047743" class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn">ISBN 978-0-12-804774-3</a>.</li>
<li><span id="CITEREFWingerson1986"></span>Wingerson L. 1986, "<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=qfak8nsMNGIC&amp;pg=PA35">America cleans up Liberty</a>", <i>New Scientist,</i> 25 December/1 January 1987, pp.&#160;31–35, accessed 1 October 2016.</li>
<li><span id="CITEREFWong_et_al.2015"></span>Wong M. Y., Hedley G. J., Xie G., Kölln L. S, Samuel I. D. W., Pertegaś A., Bolink H. J., Mosman-Colman, E., "Light-emitting electrochemical cells and solution-processed organic light-emitting diodes using small molecule organic thermally activated delayed fluorescence emitters", <i><a href="/wiki/Chemistry_of_Materials" title="Chemistry of Materials">Chemistry of Materials</a></i>, vol. 27, no. 19, pp.&#160;6535–6542, <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1021%2Facs.chemmater.5b03245">10.1021/acs.chemmater.5b03245</a>.</li>
<li><span id="CITEREFWulfsberg1987"></span>Wulfsberg G. 1987, <i>Principles of Descriptive Inorganic Chemistry</i>, <a href="/wiki/Cengage_Learning" title="Cengage Learning">Brooks/Cole Publishing Company</a>, Monterey, California, <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780534074944" class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn">ISBN 978-0-534-07494-4</a>.</li>
<li><span id="CITEREFWulfsberg2000"></span>Wulfsberg G. 2000, <i>Inorganic Chemistry</i>, University Science Books, Sausalito, California, <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781891389016" class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn">ISBN 978-1-891389-01-6</a>.</li>
<li><span id="CITEREFYadavAntonySubba_Reddy2012"></span>Yadav J. S., Antony A., Subba Reddy, B. V. 2012, "Bismuth(III) salts as synthetic tools in organic transformations", in T. Ollevier (ed.), <i>Bismuth-mediated Organic Reactions</i>, Topics in Current Chemistry 311, Springer, Heidelberg, <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9783642272387" class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn">ISBN 978-3-642-27238-7</a>.</li>
<li><span id="CITEREFYangJollyO.27Keefe1977"></span>Yang D. J., Jolly W. L. &amp; O'Keefe A. 1977, "Conversion of hydrous germanium(II) oxide to germynyl sesquioxide, (HGe)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>", <i><a href="/wiki/Inorganic_Chemistry_(journal)" title="Inorganic Chemistry (journal)">'Inorganic Chemistry</a></i>, vol. 16, no. 11, pp.&#160; 2980–2982, <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1021%2Fic50177a070">10.1021/ic50177a070</a>.</li>
<li><span id="CITEREFYousif2007"></span>Yousif N. 2007, <i>Geochemistry of stream sediment from the state of Colorado using NURE data</i>, ETD Collection for the University of Texas, El Paso, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://digitalcommons.utep.edu/dissertations/AAI3273991">paper AAI3273991</a>.</li>
</ul>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_reading">Further reading</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metals&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Further reading">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><b>Definition and usage</b></p>
<ul>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.duffus.com/jhduffus1940.htm">Duffus J. H.</a> 2002, "<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.iupac.org/publications/pac/2002/pdf/7405x0793.pdf">'Heavy metals'—A meaningless term?"</a>, <i>Pure and Applied Chemistry</i>, vol. 74, no. 5, pp.&#160;793–807, <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1351%2Fpac200274050793">10.1351/pac200274050793</a>. Includes a survey of the term's various meanings.</li>
<li>Hawkes S. J. 1997, "<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://pubs.acs.org/doi/abs/10.1021/ed074p1374">What is a "heavy metal"?</a>", <i>Journal of Chemical Education</i>, vol. 74, no. 11, p.&#160;1374, <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1021%2Fed074p1374">10.1021/ed074p1374</a>. A chemist's perspective.</li>
<li>Hübner R., Astin K. B. &amp; Herbert R. J. H. 2010, " 'Heavy metal'—time to move on from semantics to pragmatics?", <i><a href="/wiki/Journal_of_Environmental_Monitoring" class="mw-redirect" title="Journal of Environmental Monitoring">Journal of Environmental Monitoring</a></i>, vol. 12, pp.&#160;1511–1514, <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1039%2FC0EM00056F">10.1039/C0EM00056F</a>. Finds that, despite its lack of specificity, the term appears to have become part of the language of science.</li>
</ul>
<p><b>Toxicity and biological role</b></p>
<ul>
<li>Baird C. &amp; Cann M. 2012, <i>Environmental Chemistry</i>, 5th ed., chapter 12, "Toxic heavy metals", <a href="/wiki/W._H._Freeman_and_Company" title="W. H. Freeman and Company">W. H. Freeman and Company</a>, New York, <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/1429277041" class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn">ISBN 1-4292-7704-1</a>. Discusses the use, toxicity, and distribution of Hg, Pb, Cd, As, and Cr.</li>
<li>Nieboer E. &amp; Richardson D. H. S. 1980, "The replacement of the nondescript term 'heavy metals' by a biologically and chemically significant classification of metal ions", <i>Environmental Pollution Series B, Chemical and Physical</i>, vol. 1, no. 1, pp.&#160;3–26, <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1016%2F0143-148X%2880%2990017-8">10.1016/0143-148X(80)90017-8</a>. A widely cited paper, focussing on the biological role of heavy metals.</li>
</ul>
<p><b>Formation</b></p>
<ul>
<li>Hadhazy A. 2016, "<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.kavlifoundation.org/science-spotlights/cosmic-heavy-metals#.V4MEDleO7OY">Galactic 'gold mine' explains the origin of nature's heaviest elements</a>", <i>Science Spotlights</i>, 10 May, accessed 11 July 2016</li>
</ul>
<p><b>Uses</b></p>
<ul>
<li>Koehler C. S. W. 2001, "<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://pubs.acs.org/subscribe/archive/tcaw/10/i01/html/01chemch.html">Heavy metal medicine</a>", <i>Chemistry Chronicles</i>, American Chemical Society, accessed 11 July 2016</li>
<li>Morowitz N. 2006, "The heavy metals," <i>Modern Marvels</i>, season 12, episode 14, <a href="/wiki/History_(U.S._TV_channel)" title="History (U.S. TV channel)">HistoryChannel.com</a></li>
<li>Öhrström L. 2014, "<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.rsc.org/periodic-table/element/73/tantalum#podcast">Tantalum oxide</a>", <i>Chemistry World</i>, 24 September, accessed 4 October 2016. The author explains how tantalum(V) oxide banished brick-sized mobile phones. Also available as a <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.rsc.org/periodic-table/podcast/73/tantalum">podcast</a>.</li>
</ul>
